<div id="review_1196483631" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1196483631" />
    <a title="JenniferD" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2635637-jenniferd"><img alt="JenniferD" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1521134725p2/2635637.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1196483631?book_show_action=true">Feb 10, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="JenniferD" class="user" itemprop="url" name="JenniferD" href="/user/show/2635637-jenniferd">JenniferD</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780965136716" href="/book/show/73.The_Known_World">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2635637-jenniferd?shelf=2015-books">2015-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1196483631" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1515680253434176876">2.5-stars, really.<br/><br/>here is a perfect example of a books i <i>should</i> love, and yet.... i didn't. the book was a lot of work and, for me, very little reward. i think most of my issues are because of the style/structure of the novel: <br/><br/>* the third-person, omniscient narrator - this was distracting from very early on in the read. i held off judging it. i wanted to trust jones and his choice.<br/>* non-linear narrative - i don't tend to have problems with this at all, but i found it super-clunky here. also dist</span>
  <span id="freeText1515680253434176876" style="display:none">2.5-stars, really.<br /><br />here is a perfect example of a books i <i>should</i> love, and yet.... i didn't. the book was a lot of work and, for me, very little reward. i think most of my issues are because of the style/structure of the novel: <br /><br />* the third-person, omniscient narrator - this was distracting from very early on in the read. i held off judging it. i wanted to trust jones and his choice.<br />* non-linear narrative - i don't tend to have problems with this at all, but i found it super-clunky here. also distracting.<br />* the made-up references - jones would cite sources and details that seemed to lend such an air of gravitas, but none of it is actually real. (i know, i know. this is fiction. a novel. get over it. but it was just kinda weird to me. social commentary inserted into fiction happens. steinbeck did it. teju cole does it. many writers do this. i felt like jones did a crap-ton of research for this book. but then i read <a href="http://www.npr.org/templates/story/story.php?storyId=1476600" rel="nofollow noopener">this NPR piece</a>. in it, he notes he collected 2 shelves worth of books on slavery... <i>"but never got around to reading them"</i>. so this just added to my 'what the?' on the references in the novel and jones' research.)<br />* all of this combined for a really awkward flow, and convoluted storytelling. i felt like it could lose 50-100 pages and be a tighter, better story.<br /><br />in case you think maybe i am a lazy reader - i'm not, i swear! i love nothing more than a meaty, tough read. jones' story is definitely both of these things. he explores the issue of free black people owning slaves in virginia, and he gives us a large cast of active characters. but it all felt so... surface-y. we get the actions and reactions, but we don't really get the motivations or emotions. jones is navigating a morally dodgy landscape, one i would have loved to have gone into more deeply. we are given the horrors and the heartbreaks, but it all felt so detached. and, you know, maybe that is totally on purpose. maybe, for some people, detachment is the only way through such a horrible time in history.<br /><br />jones has collected some serious critical acclaim and recognition over his career. to name a few: he's won the pulitzer, the national book critic's circle award, the PEN/Hemingway award, a MacArthur genius grant, and the International IMPAC dublin literary award. he's won nearly $1 million in literary awards alone. the man knows what he's doing, and the respect he's earned is fairly universal. <i>the known world</i> is an important story. but is just shining the light on the issues jones raises enough? so now i am back to the <i>should</i>. i should love this book. (it was just okay for me.) people should read this book. (indeed. but this is not a book i will blanket recommend to all.) <br /><br />so, i don't know you guys. i am bummed over this one. and this is a terrible review/collection of thoughts. maybe i'll become more coherent with some distance and fix this up a bit - but for now i wanted to note down something here.<br /><br />(aside #1 (going to be a bit spoiler-y here): moses confused me. or, rather, his turn to all-of-a-sudden being an asshole was weird. we're going along with moses. he seems like a lovely man. then, halfway into the book, he's a wife-beater, jerkface? where did that come from, and why was he presented like that? the reveal of moses being a not nice man was sudden and odd.)<br /><br />(aside #2: worth noting (was hugely interesting to me) - the cover photo of this edition is © <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7973.Eudora_Welty" title="Eudora Welty" rel="nofollow noopener">Eudora Welty</a>! i had no idea about her <a href="http://www.smithsonianmag.com/ist/?next=/womens-history/eudora-welty-as-photographer-117044298/?all" rel="nofollow noopener">photographic prowess</a>.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="1515680253434176876" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_16818567" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/16818567" />
    <a title="Chrissie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie"><img alt="Chrissie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321799585p2/632247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/16818567?book_show_action=true">Mar 28, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chrissie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chrissie" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie">Chrissie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=usa">usa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=kirkus">kirkus</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=southern">southern</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=hf">hf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=audible-uk">audible-uk</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=2019-read">2019-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=returned">returned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=race">race</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer16818567" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16650116790031715273">Here is a book about black slave owners in the antebellum South! My interest was immediately piqued. On top of that, the book has won all sorts of prizes:<br/>*Pulitzer Prize for Fiction (2004)<br/>*International Dublin Literary Award (2005)<br/>*Anisfield-Wolf Book Award (2004) <br/>*National Book Critics Circle Award for Fiction (2003)<br/>* Hurston/Wright Legacy Award Nominee for Debut Fiction (/2004<br/>*National Book Award Finalist for Fiction (2003)<br/>I knew I had to give it a try.<br/><br/>I am glad I have read it, but to say I li</span>
  <span id="freeText16650116790031715273" style="display:none">Here is a book about black slave owners in the antebellum South! My interest was immediately piqued. On top of that, the book has won all sorts of prizes:<br />*Pulitzer Prize for Fiction (2004)<br />*International Dublin Literary Award (2005)<br />*Anisfield-Wolf Book Award (2004) <br />*National Book Critics Circle Award for Fiction (2003)<br />* Hurston/Wright Legacy Award Nominee for Debut Fiction (/2004<br />*National Book Award Finalist for Fiction (2003)<br />I knew I had to give it a try.<br /><br />I am glad I have read it, but to say I like it, would just not be true. It’s OK, so I am giving it two stars.<br /><br />There is no real central character to the story, because he dies right off the bat. It is 1855 when Henry Townsend dies. He is black. He had been a slave, but his parents had bought him his freedom. He leaves behind no children but a wife, Caldonia, a twenty-eight-year-old educated black woman born free, and his property of thirty-three slaves and fifty acres in Manchester County, Virginia. The book is about the hell that breaks loose afterwards, but really what it is about is life in the South during the antebellum era, about the mindset of Blacks and Whites of this era.<br /><br />The story is told by an omniscient narrator—who knows how each character thinks, what has happened to all of them in the past and what will happen to all of them in the future. It is this all-seeing narrator that shapes the entire feel of the story. This narrator knows everything, but he is not particularly adept at cogently telling a story. In one sentence you may switch form the present to the future and back again. In the next sentence you flip back to the past. It is easy to become confused. Character upon character is thrown at you, with little tidbits about their lives in the past, present and future. You are given an entire community of individuals—Whites and Blacks, a Native American or two, those who are free and those who are slaves, a sheriff and his deputy. The omniscient narrator is constantly restating who each one is, which is good in one sense, but the flow of the tale becomes jerky. Stop and start, backward and forward and often confusing. Use of the omniscient narrator is pushed to the extreme.<br /><br />It is kind of nice to have a character or two to guide you through a story. You do not have that here. There are a whole group of characters, characters that are hard to attach yourself to. You do not get close to any one individual. In the afterword the author points out that he wanted to draw characters that were neither all good nor all bad, each one different in their own way. You do get that here, but you fail to feel anything for any of them. What you do get is a strong sense of being one of a large community. You are there, one among many, living in the antebellum South. This does give one a hands-on feeling of life then and there and how people were thinking.<br /><br />Kevin Free narrates the audiobook. It is simple to follow, the speed is fine and he intonations are well done. The narration performance is good so I have given the narration three stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16650116790031715273" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1132312379" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1132312379" />
    <a title="Cheryl" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11255849-cheryl"><img alt="Cheryl" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565547225p2/11255849.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1132312379?book_show_action=true">Jan 07, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cheryl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cheryl" href="/user/show/11255849-cheryl">Cheryl</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=pulitzers-nominees">pulitzers-nominees</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=slave-narratives">slave-narratives</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=did-not-finish">did-not-finish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=2020-reads">2020-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=afro-american">afro-american</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1132312379" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5677370273204190022">I attended a workshop with some friends during which we all went around the room and recommended books that really moved us that year. I don't remember which book I suggested then, but I recall one woman in particular who really loved this book (or I believe she was more in love with Edward Jones' writing). At the end of the workshop we had a gift exchange raffle and she included this book as her contribution. I was the lucky recipient of this free copy. <br/><br/>I was interested in this historical novel</span>
  <span id="freeText5677370273204190022" style="display:none">I attended a workshop with some friends during which we all went around the room and recommended books that really moved us that year. I don't remember which book I suggested then, but I recall one woman in particular who really loved this book (or I believe she was more in love with Edward Jones' writing). At the end of the workshop we had a gift exchange raffle and she included this book as her contribution. I was the lucky recipient of this free copy. <br /><br />I was interested in this historical novel set in a fictional place in Virginia during the antebellum era, about a former slave who ends up owning slaves. The strangeness of the story and the angle it takes as a slave narrative is intriguing. Also appealing is the way the omniscient narration allows the reader to approach the story without the moral judgement one reserves for this kind of story, since the distance of the narration allows one to realize quickly what the story tries to showcase: how slavery has already poisoned the integrity of all involved. <br /><br />The framework of the novel is composed of anecdotal stories within stories, with pacing often interrupted for a mini scene or two, or an introduction of yet another character who may reappear later or simply disappear. Put another way, it is a book of puzzle pieces: one is added, taken away, another picked up, oh yes, there's the whole, whoops, maybe not, here goes another piece. So although I admired the writing and story setup in the beginning, I just was not in the right space to complete this novel. It has been a while since I added a book to my "did-not-finish" list; alas, this one has just made the list. This is not a failed attempt at reading this novel, however, because I remember not being able to finish Ngugi wa Thiong'o's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/57485.Wizard_of_the_Crow" title="Wizard of the Crow by Ngũgĩ wa Thiong'o" rel="nofollow noopener">Wizard of the Crow</a> only to later fall in love with his novel, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1232445.Petals_of_Blood" title="Petals of Blood by Ngũgĩ wa Thiong'o" rel="nofollow noopener">Petals of Blood</a>. With that in mind, I look forward to reading Jones' <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11752.Lost_in_the_City" title="Lost in the City by Edward P. Jones" rel="nofollow noopener">Lost in the City</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5677370273204190022" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/180732032?book_show_action=true">Jun 29, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Darwin8u" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Darwin8u" href="/user/show/2294090-darwin8u">Darwin8u</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780060557546" href="/book/show/1198384.The_Known_World">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2294090-darwin8u?shelf=2011">2011</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer180732032" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1463836451018781713">A beautifully paced novel that inverts every cliche about institution of slavery. While not exactly removing race from the issue of slavery, Jones is able to show that even among African-Americans the perniciousness of slavery could damn a generation. The best summary of this idea came from a NYTimes review:<br/><br/>'There are few certified villains in this novel, white or black, because slavery poisons moral judgments at the root. As Jones shows, slavery corrupts good intentions and underwrites bad ones</span>
  <span id="freeText1463836451018781713" style="display:none">A beautifully paced novel that inverts every cliche about institution of slavery. While not exactly removing race from the issue of slavery, Jones is able to show that even among African-Americans the perniciousness of slavery could damn a generation. The best summary of this idea came from a NYTimes review:<br /><br />'There are few certified villains in this novel, white or black, because slavery poisons moral judgments at the root. As Jones shows, slavery corrupts good intentions and underwrites bad ones, yet allows decency the odd occasion -- but only by creating such an enormous need for it.'<br /><br />The pacing and structure of this novel could be a little challenging to some, but I found that it fit the narrative perfectly. This isn't a novel that burns you with its quotable sentences, but it scars you with its images. There were periods in it where I thought I was reading a Cormac McCarthy novel. That alone is high praise.</span>

  <span id="freeText13346107446309572085" style="display:none">Disappointing. I so much wanted to like this. One star for disjointed writing and three for what I learned about the period* for an overall generous two considering how hard it was to follow. I don’t have to like the characters when I read a story but as the author is their creator I like to sense that at least <i>s/he</i> does. So many acclaimed modern authors seem preoccupied with power and domination rather than love. Presumably that is what they value... But can a preference for power ever substitute for genuine love? Isn’t the former like spiritual slavery, whereas latter akin to freedom in the fullest sense of the word? <br /><br />*Blacks in the antebellum South who gained their freedom become slave holders themselves; because slavery was so entrenched in the economy it was almost (but not entirely) impossible to conduct business without ‘servants’, i.e., the euphemism for slaves. Most freed blacks depicted in this story tried—at least initially—to treat their slaves differently than they had been treated by whites. The honesty in the story was in their discovery that the slavery system itself made human decency more difficult than they ever thought it would be. This theme was not developed.</span>

<div id="review_1539674" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1539674" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1539674?book_show_action=true">May 30, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nate" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nate" href="/user/show/62704-nate">Nate</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1539674" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10119116855684341849">Overall, the story was interesting; black families in Virginia owning their own slaves and the implications thereof. <br/><br/>The narration was told in a sweeping way that I'm sure was intended to sound like an oral history. I was willing to ignore my annoyance at not being able to gauge exactly where I was in the timeline. My problem was managing the timeline with all of the characters. I also had fun figuring out how to spot Jones's subtle segues into a new time. Toward the end of the book, I could spo</span>
  <span id="freeText10119116855684341849" style="display:none">Overall, the story was interesting; black families in Virginia owning their own slaves and the implications thereof. <br /><br />The narration was told in a sweeping way that I'm sure was intended to sound like an oral history. I was willing to ignore my annoyance at not being able to gauge exactly where I was in the timeline. My problem was managing the timeline with all of the characters. I also had fun figuring out how to spot Jones's subtle segues into a new time. Toward the end of the book, I could spot the passing of 20 years without rereading. But the beginning of the book is just a blur. The story focused most of its energy on the Townshend plantation and its workers. But all of the people on the estate were married to, children of, parents of, illegitimate parents of, cheating with people from all the other estates in the county. And once you found a character you liked you had till the end of the paragraph before a whole new character and plot point were introduced.<br /><br />I did like the book. I know many people would be fine with the flow of the narrative. I think the author could have made it easier for the reader to follow the premise and not at the expense of the oral history narration. That's all.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10119116855684341849" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1506631088" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1506631088" />
    <a title="Book Riot Community" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33447134-book-riot-community"><img alt="Book Riot Community" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1583162430p2/33447134.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1506631088?book_show_action=true">Jan 12, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Book Riot Community" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Book Riot Community" href="/user/show/33447134-book-riot-community">Book Riot Community</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1506631088" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4266107057362021190">This was the final assigned reading for my Introduction to Fiction class and it was SO GOOD. The omniscient third-person narrator takes some adjustment, but once you get into the voice, the book is grand. So many incredible characters populate Jones’ fictitious Virginia county and the discussion I’ve had on this book in class has been incredible. When considering the “Great American Novel,” The Known World should absolutely be part of that discussion. — Chris Arnone<br/><br/><br/>from The Best Books We Read In</span>
  <span id="freeText4266107057362021190" style="display:none">This was the final assigned reading for my Introduction to Fiction class and it was SO GOOD. So many incredible characters populate Jones’ fictitious Virginia county and the discussion I’ve had on this book in class has been incredible. When considering the “Great American Novel,” The Known World should absolutely be part of that discussion. — Chris Arnone<br /><br /><br />from The Best Books We Read In December: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://bookriot.com/2015/12/23/riot-round-best-books-read-december-2/">http://bookriot.com/2015/12/23/riot-r...</a></span>

<div id="review_36477090" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/36477090" />
    <a title="Will Byrnes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1526851-will-byrnes"><img alt="Will Byrnes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1291462190p2/1526851.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/36477090?book_show_action=true">Oct 29, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Will Byrnes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Will Byrnes" href="/user/show/1526851-will-byrnes">Will Byrnes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer36477090" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2375451832917367980">This tells the tale of black slave owners in the pre-Civil War south. It is an ensemble cast of characters, beautifully drawn in rich language and told with respectful remembrance. The tale jumps back and forth in time, so we know ahead the fate of some, but not all the main characters. The endings, as there are many for the diverse characters tend towards the awful, but not all fit that description. </span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('EDtDQEa4Pk4i/+PdFQjXi7K/kJW/moFDso7A6HL+snUqsNegqSfOnM4ydrZMBZF77TTV5sEH3BV+XE4AWI89iw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('F5HeUM+TPaJjkPfe0fs2v2bG5mfLnTLTK798Wx+OY6EtGkqwIAzNcI9dYrWI9nBPOU2jFLUAb4XnbfKzNf/sXw==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

Broom\'s The Yellow House tells a hundred years of her family and their relationship to home in a neglected area of one of America\'s most mythologized cities. This is the story of a mother\'s struggle against a house\'s entropy, and that of a prodigal daughter who left home only to reckon with the pull that home exerts, even after the Yellow House was wiped off the map after Hurricane Katrina. The Yellow House expands the map of New Orleans to include the stories of its lesser known natives, guided deftly by one of its native daughters, to demonstrate how enduring drives of clan, pride, and familial love resist and defy erasure. Located in the gap between the &quot;Big Easy&quot; of tourist guides and the New Orleans in which Broom was raised, The Yellow House is a brilliant memoir of place, class, race, the seeping rot of inequality, and the internalized shame that often follows. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer165851453423567537">Edward P. Jones has won the Pulitzer Prize, the National Book Critics Circle Award, the International IMPAC Dublin Literary Award, and the Lannan Literary Award for <i>The Known World</i>. He received a MacArthur Fellowship in 2004, and his first collection of short stories, <i>Lost in the City</i>, won the PEN/Hemingway Award and was short-listed for the National Book Award. His most recent collection, <i>All Aun</i></span>
  <span id="freeText165851453423567537" style="display:none">Edward P. Jones has won the Pulitzer Prize, the National Book Critics Circle Award, the International IMPAC Dublin Literary Award, and the Lannan Literary Award for <i>The Known World</i>. He received a MacArthur Fellowship in 2004, and his first collection of short stories, <i>Lost in the City</i>, won the PEN/Hemingway Award and was short-listed for the National Book Award. His most recent collection, <i>All Aunt Hagar's Children</i>, has become a bestseller. </span>
